class=\"theatre body-padding with-bottom-buffer copy-on-white\">History\n\nThis section consists of the following information:\n\nOverview  Latvia Timeline\nOverview\n\nSeveral eastern Baltic tribes merged in medieval times to form the ethnic core of the Latvian people (ca. 8th-12th centuries A.D.). The region subsequently came under the control of Germans, Poles, Swedes, and finally, Russians. A Latvian republic emerged following World War I, but it was annexed by the USSR in 1940 - an action never recognized by the US and many other countries. Latvia reestablished its independence in 1991 following the breakup of the Soviet Union. Although the last Russian troops left in 1994, the status of the Russian minority (some 26% of the population) remains of concern to Moscow. Latvia acceded to both NATO and the EU in the spring of 2004; it joined the euro zone in 2014.\n\nSource: https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/geos/lg.html\nLatvia Timeline - A Chronology of Key Events\n\n1914 - 1918: First World War\nThe summer of 1915 German army invades the Latvian territory: The formation of Latvian Rifleman Battalions to fight the German army.\nThe end of 1916: Christmas’s battles.\n1917-1918: Revolution in Russia. German army occupies Latvia completely.\n1918 November 18: The proclamation of the Republic of Latvia. Latvian National Council proclaims Latvia an independent sovereign state.\n1918 December-1920 August: Latvian Soviet government lead by Peteris Stučka operates in various places in Latvia.\n1918-1920: The War for Freedom, ending in peace treaty with Soviet Russia.\n1920 August 11: Latvian- Soviet peace treaty.\n1920 May 1: The first Latvian parliament starts work.\n1922 February 15: Latvian constitution the Satversme is ratified. The parliament Saeima starts work.\n1922: Lats the  new national currency is introduced\n1921 January 26: Latvia is officially recognized by world’s elite nations.\n1929: World economic crisis hits Latvia.\n1934 May 15: Karlis Ulmanis takes power with a coup and stops work of the Saeima and Satversme.\n1939 August 23: Nazi Germany and Soviet Union signs non-aggression pact. Latvia becomes a sphere of interest of the Soviet Union.\n1939 October 5: Soviet Union forces Latvia to sign Support agreement which permits making Soviet war bases in Latvian territory.\n1940 June 15: Soviet special units’ attacks on the Latvian border guard station at Maslenki and one other. Five people including 2 civilians were killed, 37 taken captive.\n1940 June 17: Soviet Army enters Latvia and occupies Latvia completely. Kārlis Ulmanis government loses power. He is in formally in charge until August 20. Later deported to North Caucasus.\n1940 June 20: New Soviet controlled government is installed (so called “Peoples Government”)\n1940 July 14-15 : Soviets organize so called elections of the “Peoples Saeima” with only one allowed list to vote.\n1940 July 21: “Peoples Saeima” formally makes a decision to join the Soviet Union\n1940 August 5: Latvia is officially annexed by the Soviet Union. US and other western countries don’t accept that.\n1941 July 14: Thousands of Latvian people are deported to Siberia.\n1941 July 22: Nazi Germany attacks Soviet Union and occupies Latvia.\n1941-1942: Massive extermination of Jews, Gypsies and mentally ill patients by the Nazi’s. Some 75,000 Latvian Jews were killed during the German occupation, many of them by Latvian paramilitary and police units established under the Nazis.\n1941 August 3: First Latvian division in the Red Army is formed\n1943: By Hitler’s orders the formation of Latvian Waffen-SS legion begins.\n1944-1945: Soviet Army enters Latvia. The bitter battles take place at Courland\n1945-1991: Latvia is part of the Soviet Union as the Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic.\n1945-1956: Armed Latvian National partisan resistance against the Soviet Regime takes place many parts of Latvia.\n1949 March 25: Mass deportation to Siberia.\n1956-1959: The Kruschev destalinazition policies springs up the national communist movement suppressed in 1959\n1965-1985: The Brezhnev policy of stagnation adds certain economic and political stability within Latvia\n1986: New Soviet leader Mikhail Gorbachev declares new changes in Soviet policy. The beginning of perestroika and glastnostj.\n1986: First anti-Soviet demonstrations held by nationalist and environmental activists.\n1987 July 14: “Helsinki 86” movement places flowers at the Monument of Independence commemorating the deportation of 1940. The mass movement of protests against the Soviet occupation starts.\n1988 July: The Latvian National Independence Movement is formed\n1988 October: Latvian Popular Front is formed to promote the cause of independence.\n1989 August 23: The Baltic Way takes place in all three Baltic States as a commemoration demonstration to the Molotov – Ribentrop pact.\n1989: After removing the monopoly rights of the communist party, national democratic forces get elected in the Latvian Supreme Soviet and starts course for independence\n1990 May 4: Latvian Supreme Council (parliament) declares \"de jure\" independence of Republic of Latvia. Ivars Godmanis of the People's Front becomes prime minister.\n1991 January: Soviet government tries to halt the restoration of independence. Barricades are set in Old Riga to protect the Latvian government. Numerous shootings take place at the center of Riga, the Soviets fail to quell the national movement.\n1991 March: Referendum produces large majority in favour of independence.\n1991 August 21: Following the failure of the communist coup in Moscow, Latvia restores full independence.\n1991 September: Latvia admitted to UN.\n1991 November: Citizenship law passed, giving citizenship to those who held it before Soviet occupation of 1940 and their descendants. *NOTE: This remains an issue at present with a large number of ethnic Russians living in Latvia without citizenship.\n1991 December: Soviet Union collapses and Russian Federation recognizes Latvia as a sovereign state.\n1993: Restoration of 1922 constitution.\n1993 June: First independent elections to Saeima, the 100-seat parliament.\n1993 July: Guntis Ulmanis elected president.\n1994: Russia removes all armed forces of Latvia.\n1995: Latvia admitted to Council of Europe.\n1995: Bank crisis causes economic instability.\n1996: Ulmanis re-elected.\n1999: Following end of term in office of President Ulmanis, Vaira Vike-Freiberga is elected president - the first woman president in Eastern Europe.\n2001: Riga celebrates 800th anniversary.\nRiga celebrates 800th anniversary.: Parliament votes to change election law in a bid to boost Nato membership chances. The new law omits a clause requiring parliamentary candidates to be Latvian speakers, a provision seen by human rights experts as discriminatory towards the Russian minority. Nato, EU membership.\n2002 November: Einars Repse of the New Era party invited to head centre-right coalition government following elections the previous month.\n2002 December: EU summit in Copenhagen formally invites Latvia to join in 2004.\n2003 June: Parliament elects President Vike-Freiberga to a second four-year term in office.\n2003 September: Latvian referendum vote gives strong backing to EU membership.\n2004 February: Protests as parliament passes law restricting the use of the Russian language in schools. Governing coalition collapses and Prime Minister Einars Repse resigns amid personal feuding and disputes. Indulis Emsis of Greens and Farmers Union becomes prime minister in new coalition.\n2004 March: Latvia admitted to NATO.\n2004 1 May: Latvia is one of 10 new states to join the EU.\n2004 October: Coalition government of Indulis Emsis resigns after parliament rejects draft budget. Government of his successor, Aigars Kalvitis, is approved in December.\n2005 March: Veterans of Latvia's SS brigade, which fought the Red Army in World War II, march through Riga and call on President Vike-Freiberga to pull out of Moscow war anniversary ceremony. Dozens of arrests follow clashes with rival anti-fascist protesters.\n2005 May: US President George Bush visits Riga. President Vike-Freiberga is sole Baltic president to attend Moscow Victory Day celebrations.\n2005 June: Parliament ratifies proposed EU constitution.\n2005 October: Interior Minister Eriks Jekabsons resigns, citing low budget funding for police and emergency services pay.\n2005 December: Former Prime Minister Einars Repse resigns as defence minister after criminal investigation launched into his business dealings. He denies all wrongdoing.\n2006 August: Tough new citizenship laws introduced requiring people to have good knowledge of Latvian.\n2006 October: Parties in coalition government led by Aigars Kalvitis together win parliamentary majority in general election.\n2007 March: Border demarcation treaty signed with Russia.\n2007 December: Prime Minister Kalvitis resigns, bowing to pressure over attempts to sack the country's anti-corruption chief. His coalition is returned to power in a parliamentary vote, with Ivars Godmanis as new prime minister.\n2008: World economic crisis hits Latvia.\n2008 March: Latvian bilateral accord with US on visa-free travel criticized as undermining European Union talks on the matter.\n2008 May: Latvian parliament approves European Union's Lisbon Treaty. Financial crisis.\n2008 December: International Monetary Fund (IMF) approves 1.68bn euro rescue package to help Latvia ride out severe economic slump.\n2009 January 13: Economic instability causes violent antigovernment riots in Old Riga.\n2009 February: Ruling coalition collapses amid widespread discontent over belt-tightening imposed as price of IMF rescue package. Valdis Dombrovskis of the centre-right New Era party nominated to form new government.\n2009 March: Valdis Dombrovskis is sworn in at the head of a new six-party coalition government.\n2009 June: Pro-Russian party Harmony Center secures win in Riga municipal elections. Nils Ušakovs becomes the first Russian to be Major of Riga.\n2009 June: The Central Bank spends almost a billion euros in 2009 to support the Lat currency, prevent devaluation and avoid a domino effect elsewhere in Eastern Europe.\n2009 August: Government, trade unions and employers agree deep public spending cuts aimed at saving the country from bankruptcy and getting the IMF to release a further tranche of rescue loans.\n2009 October: Government agrees to slash budget deficit in 2010 in order to meet targets imposed by EU in exchange for 7.5bn euros of rescue loans.\n2010 January: Unemployment soars to 20%, giving Latvia the highest jobless rate in the EU. Coalition fractures.\n2010 March: Largest coalition party leaves government following repeated disagreements over austerity measures, depriving PM Valdis Dombrovskis of his majority.\n2010 October: Centre-right government of PM Valdis Dombrovskis wins majority in parliamentary elections.\n2010 November: PM Dombrovskis forms government with Union of Farmers and Greens.\n2011 May 28 : Then President of Latvia Valdis Zatlers dismisses the parliament and calls for emergency elections. A few days later he himself loses the president seat by not being elected for the second term by the parliament that he dismisses. The new president Andris Bērziņš replaces him.\n2011 June: Andris Berzins is elected president.\n2011 September: Elections. Pro-Russian Harmony Centre emerges as largest party, but is excluded from coalition government.\n2012: The referendum for Russian as the second official language takes place. The majority of voters turns down the two language solution.\n2013: The Latvian government officially applies for joining Eurozone. The currency change is planned for 2014.\n2014 Jan 1: The Latvian Lat was replaced by the Euro.\n2015: Raimonds Vejonis of the Latvian Green Party is elected president.\nSources:\nhttps://latvianhistory.com/the-chronology/The short chronology of Latvian history
